720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EClicking on the ad takes users to a fake webpage designed to mimic the official Chrome Web Store. When users click \"Add to Chrome,\" they are redirected to a Google Drive link containing a zip file with the malicious extension. Attackers guide users through a process to install the extension by:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EUnzipping the file\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EGoing to their browser’s extension settings via chrome:\u002F\u002Fextensions\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EEnabling Developer Mode\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EManually loading the unpacked extension (sideloading).\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis method manipulates users into bypassing browser security checks, allowing the malware to install without detection.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E2. Malicious Extension Details: Full Access and Suspicious Permissions\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOnce installed, the malicious extension requests extensive permissions that allow it to intercept and manipulate the user’s online activities. A closer look at the extension’s manifest file reveals permissions to operate on all websites, modify network requests, and access storage and cookies. Key aspects of the manifest include:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E{\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"name\": \"Bitwarden Password Manager\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"version\": \"0.0.1\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"manifest_version\": 3,\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"background\": {\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"service_worker\": \"service-worker-loader.js\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"type\": \"module\"\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; },\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"permissions\": [\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"contextMenus\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"storage\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"cookies\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"tabs\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"declarativeNetRequest\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"webNavigation\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"webRequest\",\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \"management\"\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ]\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E}\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe extension’s \u003Cstrong\u003Eservice-worker-loader.js\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E script initiates \u003Cstrong\u003Ebackground.js\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E, the primary component driving the malicious operations. Additionally, \u003Cstrong\u003Epopup.js\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E—an obfuscated script—loads when users click the extension icon in their browser, enabling it to:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003Eaccess cookies from https:\u002F\u002Ffacebook.com, specifically seeking the c_user cookie containing the Facebook user ID.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Emanipulate the page’s DOM elements to display fake loading messages, creating an illusion of legitimate actions.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E3. Background Worker: Collecting and Exfiltrating Data\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Cstrong\u003Ebackground.js\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E script, which activates upon installation, is the core of this attack.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003Echrome.runtime.onInstalled.addListener(async details =&gt; {\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; getFacebookCookies();\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E});\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;Here’s how it operates:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECookie Harvesting\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Upon installation, \u003Cstrong\u003Ebackground.js\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E calls getFacebookCookies() to check for Facebook cookies. If found, it gathers further data using the collectData() function.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIP and Geolocation Data Collection\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The extension queries IP and location data via https:\u002F\u002Fapi.ipify.org and https:\u002F\u002Ffreeipapi.com.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EFacebook Data Extraction\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Through Facebook’s Graph API, the malware retrieves user data, including:\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EPersonal details like user ID and name\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EBusiness accounts and ad account information\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ECredit card and billing details associated with ad accounts\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOnce collected, the data is sent to a Google Script URL, which acts as the command-and-control (C2) server for the attackers. The \u003Cstrong\u003EsendData()\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E function handles data exfiltration by encoding and transmitting sensitive information.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E4. Detection and Defense Strategies\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDetecting and mitigating this attack is a challenge for cybersecurity teams due to its reliance on legitimate platforms like Facebook and Google Drive. Here are some detection ideas for security professionals:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMonitor Suspicious Permissions\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: declarativeNetRequest and webRequest permissions, paired with access to cookies, are strong indicators of potential malware.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBehavioral Signatures\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Obfuscated functions like chrome.runtime.onInstalled.addListener and calls to graph.facebook.com APIs can serve as indicators of compromise (IoCs).\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"safety-tips-for-users\"\u003ESafety Tips for Users\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETo protect yourself from similar malvertising campaigns, follow these essential security tips:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Col\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EVerify Extension Updates\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Always update extensions directly through official browser stores (e.g., Chrome Web Store) rather than by clicking on ads or third-party links.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EScrutinize Ads and Links\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Be cautious of sponsored ads on social media, especially those calling for immediate action or updates for security tools.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECheck Extension Permissions\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Before installing or updating an extension, review its permissions. Extensions requesting access to cookies, network requests, or all website data may be malicious.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EEnable Security Features\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Use browser security settings, such as disabling Developer Mode when not in use, to prevent unauthorized sideloading of extensions.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EReport Suspicious Ads\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: If you encounter misleading or malicious ads on social media, report them to the platform to help prevent the spread of similar attacks.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Fol\u003E\u003Cp\u003E6.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \u003Cstrong\u003EUse a Security Solution\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Protect yourself from malvertising and phishing attacks by using a reliable security solution like \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fen-us\u002Fconsumer\u002Ftotal-security\" rel=\"noreferrer\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBitdefender Total Security\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. These campaigns use fake advertisements to lure users into installing malicious software disguised as legitimate apps or updates.\n\nOne of the more recent campaigns Bitdefender Labs uncovered involves a fake Bitwarden extension advertised on Meta’s social media platform Facebook. The campaign tricks users into installing a harmful browser extension und",reading_time:T,url:"\u002Fblog\u002Flabs\u002Finside-bitdefender-labs-investigation-of-a-malicious-facebook-ad-campaign-targeting-bitwarden-users\u002F"},{id:"67209c9d64fc3f0560143280",title:"Unmasking the SYS01 Infostealer Threat: Bitdefender Labs Tracks Global Malvertising Campaign Targeting Meta Business Pages",slug:"unmasking-the-sys01-infostealer-threat-bitdefender-labs-tracks-global-malvertising-campaign-targeting-meta-business-pages",feature_image:"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FUnmasking-the-SYS01-Infostealer-Threat-Bitdefender-Labs-Tracks-Global-Malvertising-Campaign-Targeting-Meta-Business-Pages.jpeg",featured:d,published_at:"2024-10-30T15:59:33.000+03:00",custom_excerpt:a,html:"\u003Cp\u003EIn a world ran by advertising, businesses and organizations are not the only ones using this powerful tool. Cybercriminals have a knack for exploiting the engine that powers online platforms by corrupting the vast reach of advertising to distribute malware en masse. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhile legitimate businesses rely on ads to reach new audiences, hackers exploit these platforms to trick users into downloading harmful software. Malicious ads often seem to promote legitimate software, streaming services, or products, making it difficult for users to distinguish between safe and dangerous content.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBitdefender Labs has been tracking malvertising for years, analyzing how cybercriminals use these tactics to target people across the globe. Our latest research focuses on a growing campaign leveraging Meta’s advertising platform to spread SYS01 InfoStealer malware.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis ongoing attack impersonates popular brands to distribute malware that steals personal data, The scale and sophistication of this malvertising campaign highlight how far cybercriminals have come in weaponizing ads for their own gain.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn this article, we’ll explore how the SYS01 campaign works, the cybercriminal model that fuels it, and how hackers use hijacked accounts to keep the operation running. We’ll also offer some crucial tips on how users can protect against it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3 id=\"key-findings\"\u003EKey Findings:\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EOngoing Attack\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The malvertising campaign that has been wreaking havoc on Meta platforms for at least a month is continuously evolving, with new ads appearing daily. The SYS01 InfoStealer malware has become a central weapon in this campaign, effectively targeting victims across multiple platforms.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EElectronJs Delivery and Broadened Impersonation\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Compared to previous malvertising campaigns, the SYS01 malware is now delivered through an ElectronJs application. To maximize reach, threat actors have begun impersonating a wide range of well-known software tools, increasing the likelihood of targeting a broader user base.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EExtensive Use of Malicious Domains\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The malvertising campaign leverages nearly a hundred malicious domains, utilized not only for distributing the malware but also for live command and control (C2) operations, allowing threat actors to manage the attack in real time.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMass Brand Impersonation\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The hackers behind the campaign use trusted brands to expand their reach. Bitdefender Labs researchers noticed hundreds of ads impersonating popular video editing software like CapCut, productivity tools like Office 365, video streaming services such as Netflix, and even video games are being used to entice users. The widespread impersonation increases the likelihood of drawing in a broad audience, making the campaign highly effective.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGlobal Reach\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The scope of this attack is global, with potential victims in the millions, spanning regions such as the EU, North America, Australia, and Asia – particularly males aged 45 and above. While Meta provides some data on ad impact within the EU, there is limited transparency on how these malicious ads are affecting users outside this region, especially in the US.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EDynamic Evasion Tactics\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Threat actors continuously evolve their strategies, adapting malicious payloads almost in real time to avoid detection. Once antivirus companies detect and block a version of the malware dropper, hackers enhance obfuscation methods and re-launch new ads with updated versions.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"the-malicious-advertising-campaign\"\u003EThe malicious advertising campaign\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhile malware distributed through social media ads is not an innovation in the criminal cyberspace, a campaign that started in September stood out through the malicious samples that were distributed and because of the generic impersonation approach used by the cybercriminals. Bitdefender has previously analyzed infostealers that were distributed through ads that impersonated Artificial Intelligence software or that promised “provocative” content.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn the current campaign, the threat actors impersonate a multitude of software tools related to productivity, video or photo editing (Capcut, Canva, Adobe Photoshop), virtual private networks (Express VPN, VPN Plus) movie streaming services such as Netflix, instant messaging software such as Telegram and even video games.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure were impersonated, we have observed two approaches. The first was promoting Super Mario Bros Wonder advertisements, directly offering malicious samples.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FScreenshot-2024-10-03-154256--1--1.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1443\" height=\"536\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FScreenshot-2024-10-03-154256--1--1.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FScreenshot-2024-10-03-154256--1--1.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FScreenshot-2024-10-03-154256--1--1.png 1443w\" sizes=\"(min-width: 720px) 720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe second approach was reusing malicious domains, that impersonated a generic video game download platform (containing well known titles or recent hits like Black Myth: Wukong). The threat actors also changed the download mechanism newer samples that were similar to the ones obtained from previous ads.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fblue_games--1--1.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1533\" height=\"586\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fblue_games--1--1.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fblue_games--1--1.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fblue_games--1--1.png 1533w\" sizes=\"(min-width: 720px) 720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EConsidering the multitude of impersonated entities, the number of distributed ads, which is in the thousands, and the reach of particular ads of tens of thousands of people, it would be safe to say that this malicious advertising infrastructure could reach millions of people. Even if most of the audience does not interact with the advertisements or does not download the malicious samples, such a large potential victim pool virtually guarantees success.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"distribution-tactics-of-the-sys01-infostealer-malvertising-campaign\"\u003E&nbsp;Distribution Tactics of the SYS01 Infostealer Malvertising Campaign\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe ads typically point to a \u003Cstrong\u003EMediaFire\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E link or refer to one that allows the direct download of malicious software. The samples are obtained in the form of a \u003Cem\u003E.zip \u003C\u002Fem\u003Earchive which contains an Electron application. While the structure of the extracted archive might differ, depending on the sample, the infection method remains the same: the Javascript code embedded in the Electron app will end up dropping and executing malicious software.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads6.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"536\" height=\"235\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn many cases, the malware runs in the background while a decoy app—often mimicking the ad-promoted software—appears to function normally, making it difficult for the victim to realize they’ve been compromised.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FPicture17.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1088\" height=\"808\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FPicture17.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FPicture17.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FPicture17.png 1088w\" sizes=\"(min-width: 720px) 720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EApplications created using the Electron framework are bundled into ASAR archives (\u003Cem\u003EAtom Shell Archive Format\u003C\u002Fem\u003E). All extracted archives either contained an \u003Cem\u003Eapp.asar \u003C\u002Fem\u003Efile, or directly included the ASAR file into the main executable. The ASAR archive contains, besides the usual application icons, plenty of suspicious files:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EAnother archive that is password protected;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EA legitimate archiving\u002Funarchiving executable (for example, 7za.exe - c136b1467d669a725478a6110ebaaab3cb88a3d389dfa688e06173c066b76fcf, which is a standalone version of 7zip);\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EA \u003Cem\u003Emain.js \u003C\u002Fem\u003Efile which contains obfuscated code;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E[Optional] PowerShell scripts used as intermediaries;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EA text file containing well-known GPU models.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EUpon deobfuscating the JavaScript file, it becomes apparent that a PowerShell command is used to execute standalone 7zip, enabling the extraction of the password-protected archive.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads7.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads7.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe executed PowerShell script contains another execution command between some seemingly unrelated operations (used to avoid detection and\u002For further complicate analysis):\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads8.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" before doing this, the \u003Cem\u003Emain.js\u003C\u002Fem\u003E script checks if it is executed in a sandbox by enumerating the GPUs of the host:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads9.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe response of the PowerShell command is then cross-checked with the GPU models contained in the packed text file. If the GPU model is not in the predefined list, nothing malicious ends up being executed.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ENewer versions of the malicious main.js directly execute the unzipping process, skipping the intermediary PowerShell scripts \u003Cem\u003E(_0x3ddef2 \u003C\u002Fem\u003Eleads to the 7zip executable)\u003Cem\u003E:\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads10.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"624\" height=\"56\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads10.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads10.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EFinally, the script triggers the start of another process using a PHP interpreter and a PHP script which were part of the password-protected archive that was extracted before.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads11.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"624\" height=\"145\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads11.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads11.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe PHP samples are encoded using the IonCube Loader, making malware analysis difficult. Typically, two malicious samples are found in the extracted content: \u003Cem\u003Eindex.php \u003C\u002Fem\u003Eand \u003Cem\u003Einclude.php\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, with some samples containing \u003Cem\u003Etest.php\u003C\u002Fem\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads12.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"624\" height=\"343\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads12.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads12.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf the malware gets past the point of checking whether it ran in a sandbox, the first executed script would be \u003Cem\u003Einclude.php.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E This script enables persistence of the malware through the Task Scheduler by creating two tasks:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWDNA\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E - Ensures the periodic run of the PHP infostealer through the command \u003Cem\u003Erhc.exe php.exe index.php. \u003C\u002Fem\u003EThis task is executed every two minutes, starting with the date at which the script was first executed.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWDNA_LG \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E– At every logon of the user that executed the malware, run both the \u003Cem\u003Einclude.php \u003C\u002Fem\u003Escript (re-ensuring persistence in case it is somehow altered) and \u003Cem\u003Eindex.php.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;Analyzing a memory dump of the \u003Cem\u003Ephp.exe index.php \u003C\u002Fem\u003Eprocess reveals several interesting facts:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EThe `SYS01` string appears several times;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EThere are plenty of C2 domains that the malware can use;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003EMore domains can even be obtained through the use of Telegram bots or Google pages;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads13.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"624\" height=\"167\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads13.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads13.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads14.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"437\" height=\"147\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003EThe process uses the SQL commands that can have malicious intentions, for example `\u003Cem\u003E SELECT * FROM moz_cookies`\u003C\u002Fem\u003E.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003ESeveral URLs leading to Facebook and some of its APIs, further indicating that gaining intel about Facebook accounts, especially business pages, remains one of the goals of the infostealer.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe infostealer seems to communicate with either the hardcoded C2 domains or the ones obtained dynamically by using Telegram Bots and Google Pages by simple commands. For example, the C2 can be checked if it is up by doing a HTTP call the following way:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003Ehttps:\u002F\u002F{C2_DOMAIN}\u002Fapi\u002Frss?a=ping\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMoreover, the malware can get custom commands from the C2 server by sending commands. One example includes the \u003Cstrong\u003Eget_ck_all \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Eoperation, potential browsers for which to scrape cookies &amp; tokens\u003Cem\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002FPicture15.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"227\" height=\"535\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn this C2 response, the \u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cem\u003Eresource \u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Efield also includes several Meta Graph API calls that can be used to gain information about the Facebook accounts of the victim:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads16.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"624\" height=\"388\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads16.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2024\u002F10\u002Fads16.png 624w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIt is already becoming obvious that a core functionality of the Infostealer is to gather information about potential Facebook pages that could be used in the malicious process or sold on the dark web.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"evasion-tactics\"\u003EEvasion Tactics\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe adaptability of the cybercriminals behind these attacks makes the SYS01 Infostealer campaign especially dangerous. They use advanced evasion tactics to keep the infostealer hidden from cybersecurity tools. The malware employs sandbox detection, halting its operations if it detects it’s being run in a controlled environment, often used by analysts to examine malware. This allows it to remain undetected in many cases. In this specific case, anti-sandbox checks are made before the execution of every main component: the Javascript Unpacker, the PHP script that ensures persistence and the PHP Infostealer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen cybersecurity firms begin to flag and block a specific version of the loader, the hackers respond swiftly by updating the code. They then push out new ads with updated malware that evades the latest security measures.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"cybercriminals%E2%80%99-business-model\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECybercriminals’ Business Model\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe success of this campaign is driven by a highly structured business model that makes this malicious operation self-sustaining:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EHijacking Facebook Accounts to Power the Attack\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA key goal of SYS01InfoStealer is to harvest Facebook credentials, specifically Facebook Business accounts. Once hackers gain access to these accounts, they don’t just exploit the personal data; they use the hijacked accounts to launch more malicious ads.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWith access to Facebook’s advertising tools through compromised accounts, cybercriminals can create new malicious ads at scale without arousing suspicion. By using legitimate Facebook Business accounts, the ads appear more credible and bypass the usual security filters. This allows the attack to spread further, reaching more victims with each new wave of ads.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EScaling the Attack\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe hijacked Facebook accounts serve as a foundation for scaling up the entire operation. Each compromised account can be repurposed to promote additional malicious ads, amplifying the reach of the campaign without the hackers needing to create new Facebook accounts themselves. This is a cost-effective and time-efficient way to consistently drive traffic to malicious downloads. Being in the malvertising business isn’t just cost-effective – it also allows threat actors to stay under the radar and not rely on traditional or more obvious methods to compromise accounts, such as email phishing campaigns.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ERevenue and Data Theft\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn addition to using hijacked accounts to fund and promote their campaigns, cybercriminals can also monetize the stolen credentials by selling them on underground marketplaces, with Facebook Business accounts being highly valuable. The stolen personal information, including login data, financial info, and security tokens, can be sold to other malicious actors who may attempt to use it to fuel identity theft crimes and other attacks, turning each new victim into a revenue stream.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch2 id=\"indicators-of-compromise\"\u003EIndicators of Compromise\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMalware Hosting Domains\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fkrouki.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fkimiclass.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fgoodsuccessmedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fwegoodmedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fsocialworldmedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fsuperpackmedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fwegoodmedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Feviralmedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fgerymedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fwakomedia.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EC2 Domains\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fmusament.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fenorgutic.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Funtratem.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fmatcrogir.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fubrosive.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fwrust.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Flucielarouche.com\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ehxxps:\u002F\u002Fostimatu.top\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENote\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: This is only a short list of IOCs linked to the SYS01 campaign.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAn up-to-date, complete list of indicators of compromise is available
